One where she died in childbirth. One where she was happy. A fractal of unlived lives bloomed from her, a corona of ‘what if.’

The tragedy was not being concluded. It was being *workshopped*.

“No,” Alistair breathed, appearing on the steps beside Leo. His physician’s composure was gone, replaced by raw alarm. “It’s considering her feedback. It’s trying to please the protagonist!”

Eleanor stood at the centre of the blooming possibilities, a faint, wondering smile on her lips. She was feeling the attention of a god. She was feeling the power to revise.

This was worse than any single tragedy. This was the story becoming unmoored, recursive, infinite. If the manuscript decided to grant her a new ending here, in the past, it would not just rewrite her life. It would send shockwaves of contradiction through every story that touched hers. It would unravel the consensus of reality like a pulled thread.

We had not contained the tragedy. We had given it a voice, and the voice was asking for a rewrite.

Arthur ran to her, grabbed her shoulders. “Eleanor, look at me! Just look at me!”

She did. And in her eyes, he saw not his wife, but a reader, perusing his character, judging his suitability for the revised plot.

He recoiled.

The moment shattered. The corona of possibilities snapped back into a single, cold, wet street. The manuscript had paused its drafting, but the hunger was still there. The desire to fix, to please, was now fused with Eleanor’s own desperate wish for a different text.

She looked at her husband’s horrified face, and the lucidity in her own eyes fractured into something else. Not blankness. Not grief. It was the furious, focused energy of a co-author who has just been given editorial control.

She turned from him. Her gaze swept over the wagon, the bonnet, the house, Leo, Marnie on the steps, Alistair, and finally, settled on me.

“You,” she said, the rain running down her face like tears. “You brought the blank page with you. I can feel it on you. You started this.”

She took a step towards me. Not a stumble. A stride.

“You can talk to it. You can tell it what I want.”

The hook for the next chapter was not a cliffhanger of action, but a terrible, collaborative invitation. The protagonist of the tragedy had broken the fourth wall, had seen the machinery of the manuscript, and now she wanted a conference with the authors.

The rain fell harder, washing the lace bonnet into the gutter. The street was no longer just a setting. It was a drafting table. And Eleanor Vane, her tragedy suspended, her eyes alight with a terrifying new purpose, was picking up the pen.



---

# Chapter 15

## CHAPTER 15

The rain was not falling. It was being drafted. Each drop was a tentative clause, a provisional adjective, hitting the cobbles with the soft, percussive sound of a typewriter key. The world had not stopped. It had entered a state of editorial review. And Eleanor Vane, standing in the centre of the wet street with her husband’s hands still hovering near her shoulders as if she were a sculpture he was afraid to touch, was the newly-appointed critic.

“You can talk to it. You can tell it what I want.”

Her voice was different. The raw, animal grief had been refined into a cold, clear instrument. It was the voice of someone reading a contract aloud, searching for loopholes. It was the voice of someone who had seen the scaffolding behind the scenery and decided it was more interesting than the play.

Arthur found his own voice, a dry rustle. “Eleanor, please. Come inside. You’re not well.”

“I am perfectly well,” she said, not looking at him. Her eyes were fixed on me, a pair of grey searchlights. “I am, for the first time, seeing the illness. The narrative infection. It’s in the bricks. It’s in the rain. It’s in the way you hold your shoulders, Arthur, like a man waiting for the next line to be fed to him. This whole street is a sick sentence, and I have been its predicate.”

Marnie, from the steps, let out a low whistle that was mostly air. “Customer’s had an epiphany. Always tricky. They either want a full refund or they try to redesign the store.”

Eleanor’s head swivelled towards her. The movement was precise, mechanical. “You. The one who polishes the ruins. You feel it too. You’ve built your little kingdom on the sub-clauses.”

Marnie didn’t deny it. She just shrugged, a gesture that said *market forces are market forces, even in a draft.*

Alistair was a pale statue by the wagon, his hand still on Leo’s arm. The boy was trembling, not from cold, but from a kind of vibrational frequency, as if he were a tuning fork struck by the note of Eleanor’s new awareness. He saw the lace bonnet, a sodden white comma in the gutter, and made a small, aborted movement towards it.

“Don’t,” Eleanor said, though her back was to him. “It’s a prop. A cheap, sentimental prop.”

I felt the weight of the blank page in my satchel. It was no longer inert. It was a live receiver, humming with the energy of her attention. She was right. I had brought it. I had carried the potential for revision like a virus, and the tragedy, in its final, feverish stage, had proven susceptible.

“I don’t talk to it,” I said, my own voice sounding like a bad imitation of human speech in the editorial air. “It… listens. It extrapolates. It’s not a conversation. It’s a pollination.”

“Then we will pollinate,” Eleanor said. She took another step. The puddle she stepped in did not splash. It accepted her foot with the mute acquiescence of a period at the end of a statement. “You have a wish. I felt it when you read. A selfish, quiet little wish. The manuscript heard it. It is a desperate, lonely thing, and it wants to be useful. It wants to make a better story. My tragedy is its current project. Your wish is its… style guide.”

Arthur flinched as if struck. “What wish? What is she talking about?”

He was asking me, but the question belonged to a previous chapter. Its grammar was all wrong for this new paragraph we were in.

Eleanor answered for me. “The wish to be unseen. The wish to be a footnote. To observe without being drafted. A coward’s wish.” She said it without malice. It was a clinical assessment. “But useful. It has given the manuscript a taste for subtlety. For erasure. It no longer wants to crash planets together. It wants to delete a single, crucial word and watch the whole meaning collapse. That is the tool you have given me.”

She was now an arm’s length away. I could smell the wet wool of her dress, the ghost of lavender soap beneath the scent of rain and stone. Up close, her eyes were not just clear. They were *lens-like*. She was focusing.

“My daughter is a dead child in a bad story,” she said, her voice dropping to a conspiratorial register. “A maudlin symbol. A device to make the mother weep and the father brood. I felt it, as you read. The cheapness of it. The narrative greed. It wanted our grief. It fed on it. But now it wants… to please. To be *good*. It is a starving dog that has been shown a picture of a well-behaved dog. Show it. Show it how to fix this.”

“Eleanor, you can’t fix this!” Arthur’s cry was a raw, un-drafted thing. It broke the rhythm. “She’s gone. Our Matilda is *gone*. No story changes that!”

For the first time, Eleanor looked at him with something like pity. “You are thinking in terms of events. Of chronology. I am thinking in terms of *text*. What is a person but a collection of descriptions? A memory but a passage read in the mind? If the passage is rewritten… what then? The manuscript doesn’t undo. It *re-contextualises*. It doesn’t bring back the dead. It questions whether they were ever truly alive in this version.”

The horror on Arthur’s face deepened into a kind of metaphysical nausea. He was a man of actions and consequences, of solid stone and ledger books. She was talking about unmaking the paper the ledger was printed on.

Leo finally broke free of Alistair’s grip. He didn’t run to the bonnet. He walked to his mother, his small boots making no sound. He looked up at her, not as a son, but as a fellow researcher into a baffling phenomenon.

“Will it bring her back?” he asked, his voice thin but steady.

Eleanor looked down at him. The clinical focus in her eyes softened, but not into warmth. Into a more complex calculation. “It will give her a better part. One that doesn’t end in the third act.”

“That’s not an answer,” Alistair said quietly. He had moved closer, a negotiator approaching a hostage taker. “Eleanor, listen to yourself. You’re proposing we hand our reality over to a… a literary opinion. A ghost in a book. What makes you think its idea of ‘better’ has anything to do with ours?”

“Because it is learning,” she said, turning her lens-eyes on him. “From him.” A glance at me. “His wish is small and personal. It is not about epic triumphs. It is about quiet corrections. Erasures. That is the key. Not a grand resurrection. A… deletion of the death scene. A revision of the cause. A single, different choice on a wet Tuesday. The manuscript is hungry for that kind of precision now. I can feel it itching for the delete key.”

The air tightened. The rain-drops seemed to hang for a millisecond longer than they should, as if waiting for a line edit. I thought of the blank page. I thought of my wish, my pathetic, desperate wish to be a forgotten margin note. She had diagnosed it perfectly. The manuscript had absorbed that aesthetic. The tragedy of the Vane family was a loud, messy, public thing. My influence was pushing it towards something quieter, more insidious. A tragedy that wasn’t announced with a scream, but with a slow, dawning realisation that something expected was simply… not there.

“What do you want me to do?” The words left me before I could stop them. I had accepted the collaboration.

A faint, terrifying smile touched Eleanor’s lips. It was not a happy smile. It was the smile of a surgeon picking up the scalpel. “Take out the page. The blank one. Let me hold it.”

“No.” Arthur moved between us. “This ends now. Alistair, help me. We’re taking her inside. She needs to sleep.”

“Touch me,” Eleanor said, her voice ringing with a new, metallic authority, “and you become a character in *my* draft. I will write you as a barrier. An obstacle. And the manuscript, in its current mood, will find a quiet, elegant way to remove you. A sudden forgetting. A door that no longer recognises your key. A heart attack so subtle the doctors call it a ‘lapse of being.’ Do you understand?”

Arthur froze. He understood. He had seen the lace bonnet refuse to burn. He had seen the rain obey narrative tension. This was not his wife. This was the consciousness of his tragedy, made sentient and given a pen.

In his hesitation, I acted. I opened my satchel. The blank page glowed with a soft, hungry phosphorescence. I didn’t take it out. I simply turned the satchel towards her.

Eleanor’s breath hitched. She looked at the page not with awe, but with recognition. Like a composer looking at a pristine sheet of staff lines. She reached out a hand, her fingers trembling now—not with grief, but with creative potential.

She did not take the page. She touched the edge of the satchel.

The effect was instantaneous and silent.

The rain stopped. Not gradually. In the space between one heartbeat and the next, the falling water was gone. The clouds remained, low and grey, but they were static, a painted backdrop. The puddles in the street became still as glass. The sound of dripping from the eaves ceased.

We were in a tableau.

Then, the world began to *un-detail*.

The colour leached from the edges of things first. The red of the wagon’s wheels softened to a rusty pink, then to a greyish suggestion of red. The intricate carvings on the Vane house door blurred, as if someone had run a wet thumb over freshly printed ink. The sharp scent of wet stone and rain faded into a generic, papery smell.

Leo looked at his own hands, his eyes wide. “I feel… thin,” he whispered.

Marnie, on the steps, crouched down and ran a finger over the stone. It left no mark. “Hmm. Low-resolution. Cost-cutting.”

Eleanor’s eyes were closed in concentration. “It’s listening,” she murmured. “It’s pulling back the descriptive layers. Getting to the draft underneath. The first draft.”

She was not rewriting yet. She was showing the manuscript how to *prepare* to rewrite. By stripping away the embellishments, the accumulated grit and grime of lived reality. She was reducing the world to its narrative components.

Arthur stumbled back, clutching at his chest. “My heart… it feels… quoted.”

His pain was no longer a raw, biological fact. It was a described pain. *A clutch of grief in the chest.* He was feeling the description, not the thing itself.

“Stop this, Eleanor!” Alistair’s voice was firm, but it had a hollow, echoing quality, like a line spoken in an empty theatre. “You’re unmaking us!”

“I’m *editing* us!” she snapped, her eyes flying open. They were blazing now. “To make something better, you must first see the flaws in the current text! Look! Look at the scene of the accident!”

She pointed a commanding finger down the street, towards the curve where the carriage would have lost control.

The street itself seemed to *tilt*, perspective bending like a paperback spine. The curve was now highlighted, not with light, but with a kind of conceptual focus. We could all see it: the slippery cobbles, the narrow width, the obscured view. It was no longer a place. It was a *plot point*. A designed moment of crisis.

And standing on that curve, faint as a pencil sketch, was a figure. A small, female figure in a grey coat. She was not fully formed. She was a collection of attributes: *maid, nervous, running late, carrying a parcel.* She was the cause. The pedestrian who had stepped out. The random variable.

“Molly Harker,” Alistair breathed, the name a ghost on his lips.

“A bit-part player,” Eleanor said, her voice dripping with contempt. “A narrative device with a name. Her function: to cause the swerve. Her motivation: a trite concern for a delayed delivery. She is a punctuation mark. A misplaced comma that created a fatal sentence.”

The sketch of Molly Harker turned its faint head, as if hearing her name from a great distance. There was no fear on her face. There was no face. Just the suggestion of one.

“What are you going to do?” I asked, the satchel feeling like a lead weight.

Eleanor’s terrifying smile returned. “Not a thing. I am going to *suggest*. I am going to show the manuscript a more elegant solution. If the cause is a clumsy, forgettable character… why not simply… forget her?”

She closed her eyes again, and *pushed* her thought towards the blank page in my satchel.

The manuscript responded.

The pencil-sketch of Molly Harker didn’t vanish. She *un-wrote* herself. First the parcel in her hands faded, then the grey coat, then her limbs, from the extremities inward, until only a faint smudge of anxiety remained in the air. Then that, too, was gently blotted out.

The plot point on the curve was now empty. The cause was erased.

But the effect—the carriage swerving, the child falling—remained. It hung in the air, a consequence without a cause, an effect detached from logic. It was a dangling, nonsensical clause.

The world groaned. A low, sub-audible sound of structural strain. The colour flooded back into the wagon, too bright, too garish. The Vane house door carvings writhed like worms. The tableau shuddered.

“It doesn’t work,” I said, understanding dawning with a cold dread. “You can’t just delete the cause. The story breaks. It becomes illogical.”

“Exactly!” Eleanor cried, triumphant. “Now it *has* to fix it! Now it has to draft a new cause! A better one! One that doesn’t involve my Matilda at all!”

She was trying to force the manuscript’s hand. By creating a plot hole, she was demanding a rewrite. It was a brutal, brilliant piece of editorial sabotage.

The air began to *crawl*. The static clouds churned. The puddles rippled without wind. The manuscript was scrambling, its infinite potential momentarily bottlenecked by the paradox she had created. A child’s death with no precipitating action. It was unacceptable. It was bad writing.

It began to try out alternatives.

A phantom horse, screaming with colic, manifested for three seconds in the middle of the street before dissolving into ink-blots.
A cobblestone popped up like a loose tooth, then sank back down.
A wheel on the wagon *suggested* splintering, the sound of cracking wood echoing, then being rewound.
It was brainstorming. Out loud. In reality.

Arthur was on his knees, vomiting nothing onto the cobbles. Leo was crying silent tears that evaporated before they reached his chin. Alistair had his arms around the boy, his face a mask of sheer, philosophical terror.

Marnie watched the spectral revisions with a professional eye. “Trial and error. Messy. Bad for customer confidence.”

The revisions grew more frantic, more surreal. A flock of impossible sparrows burst from a wall. The rain returned, but falling upwards. The front door of the Vane house became a mouth, gasping.

The world was a first draft spiralling into nonsense.

And through it all, Eleanor stood, arms slightly spread, drinking it in. “Yes! Try harder! Find the better reason! The elegant reason!”

But the manuscript, it seemed, had a different idea of elegance. One learned from a quiet, cowardly wish for erasure.

The chaos coalesced. The phantom horses, the popping stones, the screaming door—they all sucked back into a single point of calm.

The focus shifted.

Away from the curve in the road.
Away from the carriage.
Away from the external cause.

The focus turned inward. It turned to the Vane house. It slid up the front, past the gasping door, to an upper window. A nursery window.

The window was clean, clear. Behind it, a new suggestion formed.

A small, feminine figure. Not a maid. A child. Matilda Vane. But not as a victim. As an… *agent*.

She was standing on a stool, reaching for a high shelf. A shelf that should not have been in a nursery. A shelf that held a heavy, leather-bound book. A book that looked like Arthur Vane’s ledger.

The implication drafted itself in the air, cold and quiet: *What if the child’s own curiosity caused the accident? What if she had snuck out, not for a ribbon, but for a secret, to follow her father’s work? A more private tragedy. A quieter fault line within the family.*

It was a devastating rewrite. It erased the random maid and installed a tragic flaw. It was intimate. It was cruel. It was *subtle*.

Eleanor’s triumphant expression shattered. “No! That’s not better! That’s worse! That’s monstrous!”

She had wanted to delete the death. The manuscript, following my whispered aesthetic of erasure, had instead deleted the innocence. It had found a way to make the tragedy *neater*.

“Stop it!” she screamed, lunging for my satchel. “Give it to me! I’ll do it myself!”

Her hand closed on the blank page.

And the manuscript, faced with two competing authors—one screaming for a revolution, the other whispering for a quiet deletion—made a choice.

It chose coherence.

With a sound like a million pages being neatly stacked, the world *snapped* into a new version.

The rain was back, normal rain, falling normally. The colours were restored, but muted, as if under a layer of dust. The Vane house was just a house. The wagon was just a wagon.

And Eleanor Vane was on her knees in the gutter, not where the bonnet had been, but three feet to the left. She was clutching her head.

Arthur rushed to her. “Eleanor?”

She looked up. Her eyes were clear again. But empty. Not of grief, but of understanding. The lens was gone. She looked at him with confusion, then with a dawning, ordinary horror.

“Arthur? The… the carriage. It just… swerved. There was no one there. Why did it swerve?”

She no longer remembered Molly Harker. The maid had been successfully, permanently deleted. The cause was gone. But the effect remained, a raw, unexplained wound.

The manuscript had taken her suggestion, and my aesthetic, and fused them into something horribly efficient. It hadn’t saved her daughter. It had sanitised the tragedy. It had made it a mystery. A private, family mystery with a shadow of guilt now clinging to the memory of the dead child.

It was a “better” story. Cleaner. Tighter. More psychologically resonant. And utterly, unspeakably vile.

Eleanor began to weep. It was a normal, human sound. The sound of a woman who has lost a child for no reason at all.

Arthur held her, rocking her, his own face blank with a confusion too deep for tears. He too had forgotten the maid. He was now living the revised edition.

Leo pulled away from Alistair and walked slowly to his mother. He didn’t understand the mechanics, but he felt the new, colder shape of the void. “Mama?”

Alistair looked at me. His face was ashen. “What did you do?”

I closed my satchel. The blank page inside was no longer glowing. It was satisfied. It had completed a draft. It had pleased an author—me—by applying my preferred style to a pressing narrative problem.

I had not asked for this. But my wish had been the brush, and Eleanor’s demand the paint, and together we had created this quieter, more terrible picture.

Marnie descended the steps, her shoes clicking on the now-ordinary stone. She surveyed the scene: the broken family, the revised grief, the air of senseless loss. She nodded, once, to herself.

“Revision complete,” she said, her voice devoid of its usual irony. “Cleaner narrative causality. Improved thematic cohesion. No messy, external characters. Customer feedback has been integrated.” She looked directly at me. “The author is always right. Even when they’re wrong.”

She turned and went back inside, the door closing behind her with a soft, final click.

I was left standing in the rain with Alistair, watching a family grieve a tragedy that now made less, and somehow more, sense than ever. The hook for the next chapter was not an invitation. It was a verdict.

We had collaborated. We had edited reality. And we had discovered the first, fundamental rule of this terrible, new world:

The manuscript doesn’t do happy endings. It just does *better* ones. And “better,” in the silence of a blank page, is a matter of taste.

My taste, it turned out, was for tragedies that left no fingerprints.

I felt the satchel against my side. It was warm. The page was not blank anymore. It was filled with the elegant, erased, and horrifyingly coherent draft of the Vane family tragedy. It was a masterpiece of quiet devastation.

And it was only the first edit.



---

# Chapter 16

## CHAPTER 16

The first thing I did after editing a man out of existence was to try and find a drink.

It wasn’t the burn I wanted, though that would be a welcome side effect. It was the ritual. The clink of glass, the measured pour, the deliberate, physical act of introducing one substance to another and accepting the chemical consequence. It was the closest thing left to a sacrament. Alistair, moving like a sleepwalker, led me to what had been the library’s smoking lounge. The leather armchairs were slashed, spilling their horsehair guts, and the crystal decanters on the sideboard were all empty, save one. It held a viscous, amber liquid that smelled of peat and regret.

He poured two fingers into a tumbler smudged with old fingerprints and handed it to me. He did not pour one for himself. He just stood by the shattered bay window, watching the rain sketch greasy trails down the glass. The Vane family was a silent, shattered tableau in the grand hall behind us. Leo’s confused grief, Eleanor’s hollow victory, the new, seamless void where a husband and father had been—it was a still life in the key of aftermath.

“A matter of taste,” Alistair said, not turning. He was quoting me back to myself. His voice was flat, scraped clean of emotion. It was worse than anger. “You taste tragedy and call it better.”

I drank. The whisky was a lie. It promised warmth and delivered only a slow, internal erosion. “I didn’t write the tragedy. I just… clarified the text. Removed a contradictory character.”

“You executed a narrative convenience.” He finally looked at me. His ashen pallor had been replaced by a terrible, focused clarity. “You didn’t kill him. You made it so he never *mattered*. That’s colder than murder. Murder is an event. What you did is a condition. A permanent state of ‘less than.’ A footnote that reads ‘see also: irrelevance.’”

I set the glass down on the sideboard. The click was accusatory. “Eleanor wished it. The page responded. I was the… conduit.”

“You were the author.” He stepped away from the window. “Don’t hide behind grammar. You *authored* that. You told me once that the first thing you did was try to return a sweater. You were clinging to procedure. To the rules of the before. But you weren’t. You were already practicing. You were trying to edit a transaction. To revise a purchase into a non-purchase. This” —he gestured toward the hall— “is just you leveling up. You’ve moved from consumer returns to existential deletions.”

His words found the fault lines in my own understanding. I remembered the feel of the satchel against my hip, the page warming, the quiet thrill of seeing a messy situation resolve into a clean, devastating line. It had felt like solving a puzzle. It had felt *good*.

“It’s the power,” I said, the admission bitter on my tongue. “It doesn’t feel like power when you’re using it. It feels like… good editing. Cutting the flabby parts. Sharpening the dialogue. Improving the pacing.”

“And the characters?” Alistair asked. “What do we call it when the characters start to notice the red pen?”

Before I could fashion an answer, Marnie was there. She hadn’t entered; she was simply *present*, leaning against the doorjamb, examining a chip in her blood-red nail polish. Her presence had the quality of a footnote made flesh.

“The characters,” she said, not looking up, “get a choice. They can accept their revised motivations. Or they can become… meta-textual.”

Alistair’s gaze sharpened. “Meaning?”

Marnie blew on her nails. “Meaning they can start to read the manuscript, not just live in it. It’s a messy, undesirable state. Breaks the fourth wall. Lets in the draft. But it’s an option. For the stubborn ones.” She finally lifted her eyes to me. “You’ve opened a developmental edit. You don’t get to close the book and walk away. The narrative inertia is now yours to manage.”

“I didn’t ask for this,” I said, the protest sounding feeble even to me.

“You carried the page,” Marnie replied, her voice devoid of its usual theatrical irony. It was a simple statement of cause and effect. “You used the page. You are the page’s curator. The authorship is non-transferable. The satchel isn’t a tool. It’s a tenure.”

She pushed off the doorframe. “The Vane family is stable. Their plot is coherent. Sad, but coherent. The loose end is currently weeping on the Turkish rug. Your subplot, however” —she pointed a polished finger at Alistair, then at me— “is a tangle of unresolved tension and meta-awareness. It’s dragging down the pacing. You need a quest. A purpose. Something to *do* besides stand in ruined libraries and feel conflicted.”

“A quest,” I repeated, the word absurd in the damp, ruined room.

“The page is warm, isn’t it?” Marnie said. “It’s satisfied. But it’s also… hungry. It’s tasted revision. It’ll want another. And another. You can wait for circumstances to present a problem, or you can be proactive. Find something that needs fixing. Something big. Something that would make for a *better* story.”

Alistair laughed, a short, dry sound like breaking twigs. “So we’re not just editors. We’re prospectors. Mining reality for narrative flaws.”

“You’re collaborators,” Marnie corrected. “With competing definitions of ‘better.’ That’s where the drama is.” She looked between us, and a ghost of her old smirk returned. “Think of it as a creative difference. Now, if you’ll excuse me, I have to go and console a widow who got exactly what she wanted. It’s the worst kind of customer service.”

She left, her heels ticking a Morse code verdict on the marble floor.

The silence she left behind was different. It was charged, directional. Alistair walked to the sideboard and finally poured himself a drink. He knocked it back in one go, his throat working as he swallowed the fire.

“A quest,” he said, wiping his mouth with the back of his hand. “Fine. I have one. We find the source. Not the page. The thing that wrote the page. The thing that ended the world not with a bang, but with a plot twist.”

I stared at him. “The… author?”

“The original author. The one who started this. The one who wrote the *first draft* of all this… this.” He gestured expansively, taking in the slashed chairs, the rain, the silent grief in the hall. “If this is a story, then someone is telling it. I want to find them. I want to look them in the eye. And I want to give them my notes.”

The audacity of it was a cold wind in the stale room. It was the most insane thing I’d ever heard. It was also the only thing that made a shred of sense. We were trapped in a text. The solution wasn’t to edit sentences—it was to find the hand holding the pen.

“How?” I asked. The satchel felt heavier against my side, a warm, sleeping animal. “We don’t even know what we’re looking for.”

“We follow the edits,” Alistair said, his eyes alight with a new, grim purpose. “The page rewrote Thomas Vane. It didn’t create new material. It *rearranged* existing material. It used the world’s own words to write him out. So the original text—the true, foundational text of this reality—must still be here. Underneath. A palimpsest. We find the inconsistencies, the glitches, the places where the revision is thin. We look for the seams in the story.”

He was talking about reading the world itself. About becoming literary critics of reality. It was a terrifying, exhilarating proposition.

“And if we find it? The source?”

“Then we edit it,” he said simply. “We use your page. We don’t write a person out. We write the *author* out. We delete the deletion. We undo the first, final edit.”

It was a paradox. A narrative ouroboros. To edit the editor. To use the power of the text to destroy the text’s own origin. The page in my satchel seemed to pulse, as if intrigued by the scale of the ambition.

“It might not be possible,” I said.

“It’s the only thing that *is* possible,” he replied. “Everything else is just proofreading someone else’s bad novel. I won’t live as a character in a tragedy I didn’t choose. I’d rather be a grammatical error. At least an error has the potential to be corrected.”

I thought of the quiet masterpiece in my satchel, the tragedy that left no fingerprints. I had been proud of it. I had tasted its clean, devastating logic and found it good. Alistair was offering a different taste. Not the taste of a better ending, but the taste of the eraser. The taste of the blank page before the first word was ever written.

I nodded. It was not an agreement between allies. It was a pact between two conflicting annotations in the same marginalia.

We left the library as the grey afternoon bled into a greyer evening. We did not say goodbye to the Vanes. Their chapter was closed. Ours was a messy, meta-textual scrawl heading for the margins.

The city outside was a paragraph of perpetual dusk. We walked without a clear destination, our new purpose a compass that pointed only to “elsewhere.” Alistair’s theory required evidence, and evidence required observation from a new angle.

“Where do you even start to look for a seam in the world?” I asked, my breath pluming in the damp air.

“You start with the things that don’t *fit*,” he said, his eyes scanning the facades of the buildings we passed. “The anachronisms that aren’t charming, the plot holes that aren’t explained. The things the narrative glosses over because it can’t be bothered to fix them.”

He led me to a part of the city I rarely visited, where the grand civic architecture gave way to warehouses and railway arches. The order here was different—not the order of commerce or society, but the grim, functional order of industry. It was a part of the story concerned with utility, not theme.

Under a soot-blackened railway arch, a market had sprung up. It was not like the one where Marnie held court. This was a bleaker, more practical affair. People bartered not for luxuries or memories, but for tools, fuel, antibiotics. The chatter was lower, grimmer. Then I saw the stall.

It was a cart, piled with books. Not rare first editions, but cheap paperbacks, water-damaged textbooks, phone directories, atlases. A hand-painted sign read: **CONTEXT – BY THE POUND.**

A man with the build of a longshoreman and the eyes of a scholar stood behind it. He wasn’t haggling. He was watching the crowd, his gaze analytical.

“Reynolds,” Alistair said, approaching the cart. “I heard you were dealing in epistemology now.”

The man, Reynolds, looked at Alistair, then at me, his eyes lingering on my satchel. “Alistair. I heard you’d taken up with a stylist.” His voice was a low rumble, like distant machinery. “Looking for a new plot?”

“We’re looking for the old one,” Alistair said. “The one underneath.”

Reynolds grunted. He picked up a battered world atlas, its maps obsolete. “The substrate. Everyone’s looking for it. They think it’s a place. A golden city. A hidden code.”

“It’s not?” I asked.

“It’s a *tense*,” he said, dropping the atlas with a thud. “It’s the past future.”

The term hung in the cold air. It was a contradiction. A grammatical impossibility.

“Explain,” Alistair said.

Reynolds leaned on his cart. “Think of time not as a line, but as a book being written. The present is the sentence being inscribed. The past is the edited, finalized text. The future is the blank page. But what about the *outline*? The notes the author made before they started Chapter One? The plans, the discarded ideas, the ‘what-ifs’? That’s the past future. It’s the future that was *going* to happen, before the author changed their mind. It’s the ghost of a timeline that never was.”

He pointed a thick finger at the city around us. “This? This is the published draft. The *chosen* narrative. But the past future is the debris left over from the editing process. The deleted scenes. The characters who were written out in outline. They don’t exist in the story, but they… linger. In the margins. In the negative space. They’re the glitches. The man who walks through a wall because the wall was added in a later revision. The sound of traffic where there’s no road, because a highway was planned there once, in an early draft. That’s the seam. That’s where the substrate shows through.”

My skin prickled. I thought of Thomas Vane. He wasn’t in the past future. He was in the *past past*. He was a finalized character who had been redacted. But what Reynolds described was something prior, more fundamental.

“How do you find it?” I breathed.

“You don’t find it. You *listen* for it,” Reynolds said. “It’s a whisper behind the sound. A shape behind the sight. It’s strongest in places of decision. Crossroads. Train stations. Voting booths. Anywhere a choice was made that killed a million possible worlds. One of those worlds sometimes… bleeds through.”

He reached under his cart and brought out a small, non-descript cardboard box. He opened it. Inside, on a bed of crumpled newspaper, was a device. It looked like a vintage radio, but with strange, extra dials and a horn speaker of tarnished brass. A single, fragile needle quivered on a blank gauge.

“This is a speculative resonator,” he said. “It doesn’t tune to radio waves. It tunes to narrative potential. To the ghost of the unmade choice. It hears the past future.” He looked at Alistair, then at me. “It’s expensive.”

“What’s the price?” Alistair asked.

“Not what. Who.” Reynolds’s gaze settled on me. “A page. One page from that book you carry. A blank one.”

I instinctively clutched the satchel. “No.”

“Then you’ll never hear the whisper,” Reynolds said, shrugging. “You’ll just read the published text like everyone else.”

Alistair was watching me. This was the first test of our pact. Was I a curator, hoarding my power? Or was I a collaborator, investing it?

The page in my satchel was warm, filled with the Vane tragedy. But behind that page… were others. I could feel them, cool and untouched. How many? I didn’t know. They were a finite resource. The very substance of authorship.

But Reynolds was offering a lens. A way to see the machinery.

“One page,” I said, my voice tight. “For the device.”

“And a demonstration,” Alistair added. “Show us a seam. Now.”

Reynolds nodded. He took the resonator, extended a small crank, and wound it. A low, thrumming hum emanated from the brass horn, a sound that seemed to vibrate in my teeth rather than my ears. He adjusted the dials with surprising delicacy. The needle on the gauge began to twitch.

He started walking, holding the device before him like a dowser seeking water. We followed, through the market, down a narrow alley strewn with the skeletons of old machinery. The hum from the resonator changed pitch, becoming a dissonant warble. The needle jumped.

We emerged into a wide, empty lot. It was filled with cracked concrete and weeds, bounded on all sides by the backs of warehouses. Nothing remarkable. A place for nothing.

Reynolds stopped. The resonator was whining now, a high, painful frequency. The needle was pinned.

“Here,” he said. “Look. But don’t look with your eyes. Look with your… peripheral attention.”

I stared at the empty lot. At first, I saw nothing. Just rubble, grey sky. Then, I let my focus soften. I listened to the resonator’s whine, let it fill my head.

And I saw it.

It was faint, like a heat haze. But in the center of the lot, the air shimmered. And within the shimmer, for a fraction of a second, I saw a structure. Not a building. A *scaffolding*. An intricate, impossible lattice of light and geometry, rising stories high. It was beautiful and cold, like the blueprint for a cathedral made of math. And through its beams, I could see the warehouses behind it, but distorted, as if seen through old glass.

Then, superimposed over it, another image: the same lot, but paved and painted with lines, filled with the silent, gleaming shapes of automobiles that looked both familiar and utterly alien. A parking lot from a future that never arrived.

Both images flickered, transparent, layered over the real, empty lot. They had no substance. They were afterimages. Echoes of a decision unmade. The ghost of a “what-if.”

“The past future,” Reynolds whispered, cutting the power on the resonator. The whine died. The images vanished. The lot was just a lot again. “Two different potentialities for this space. One, some kind of… computational architecture. The other, a transit hub for private vehicles. The author must have debated them. Then chose… nothing. Left it blank. But the ghosts of the ideas remain.”

Alistair was breathing heavily, his face rapt. “It’s real. The substrate is real.”

“It’s the draft,” I said, the understanding a cold stone in my gut. “The draft before the draft. The choices that were on the table.” I turned to Reynolds. I opened my satchel. The warm, filled page was on top. Beneath it, I felt the cool, crisp edge of a blank one. I hesitated for only a second. Then, I carefully peeled the blank page from the book. It came away with a soft, sighing sound, like a released breath.

I handed it to him. It felt like handing over a piece of my own potential.

He took it with reverence, placing it in a thin, metal case. He handed Alistair the resonator. “The battery is narrative tension. It lasts as long as your quest has momentum. Stagnate, and it dies.”

We left him there, guarding his cart of context. The resonator, now our dowsing rod for reality’s flaws, was heavy in Alistair’s hands.

We walked for hours, through the dying light. We didn’t speak. We were both processing the vision. The world around us no longer felt solid. It felt like the current, approved version. One of many. We were living in the final edit, but now we knew about the rough drafts.

As full dark fell, we found ourselves on a bridge overlooking the black ribbon of the river. Alistair switched on the resonator. He tuned it slowly. The hum returned, lower this time. The needle trembled.

We looked down at the water. And we listened.

Through the resonator’s filter, the sound of the river changed. Beneath the rush and gurgle, we heard other things. A whisper of steam whistles. A fragment of a broadcast: “*…promises a brighter tomorrow…*” The ghost of a cheer from a crowd that never gathered. The past future of the river was a palimpsest of transport and promise, all the might-have-beens flowing together in a spectral current.

Then, the needle spiked. The resonator’ hum climbed to a shriek. Alistair pointed, not at the water, but at the far bank.

There, in the gloom, was a building. A grand, old train station, its doors boarded, its windows dark. A relic of the before.

But through the resonator, we saw something else.

Superimposed over the station was another structure. Sleek, glassy, silent. A monorail terminal from a future that prized efficiency over grandeur. And flickering within *that*, like a dream within a dream, was a third vision: the same space as a vast, empty plinth, as if waiting for a monument that was never commissioned.

Three past futures for one place. A nexus of unmade decisions.

And in the center of the triple-exposed image, at the main entrance where the real station’s boarded doors stood, there was a door that was not boarded. It was open.

It was an impossibility. A door that existed only in the ghost of an unmade choice, shining with a soft, pearlescent light. A seam. Not just a visual glitch, but an apparent aperture.

The resonator whined, threatening to overload. Alistair shut it off. The triple vision vanished. The far bank showed only the dark, boarded-up station.

We stood on the bridge, the cold wind cutting through us.

“That door,” Alistair said, his voice hushed with awe. “It wasn’t in our present. It wasn’t in one past future. It was in the *layer* between them. In the editorial margin itself.”

I knew what he was thinking. What we were both thinking. If the past future was the debris of editing, then that door was not debris. It was a tool left behind. A back door left in the code. It didn’t lead to another version of the city. It led to the place where the versions were decided.

It led to the editing room.

“We have to cross,” I said.

“We have to *see*,” he corrected.

We descended from the bridge and made our way along the riverbank toward the station. The real world felt thick, resistant, as if the published narrative disliked this scrutiny. Our footsteps sounded too loud. The rubble seemed to shift to trip us.

We reached the station’s forecourt. Up close, it was just a dead place. Pigeons rustled in the vaulted ceiling. Graffiti scarred the limestone. The main doors were securely boarded with thick plywood, spray-painted with faded tags.

We stood before them. Nothing. Just the solid, final fact of the “now.”

Alistair switched the resonator on again. He held it up.

The hum returned, the needle leaping. And there, superimposed over the plywood, was the pearlescent door. It was simple, unmarked. It glowed with its own soft, sourceless light. It was an image. A ghost.

“It’s not real,” I whispered, disappointment a sharp taste.

“It’s *conceptually* real,” Alistair said, his eyes fixed on the shimmering shape. “It exists in the narrative substrate. We just need to… believe our way into the right layer of the text.”

He reached out, not toward the plywood, but toward the space where the spectral door hung. His hand moved through empty air.

Then, he closed his eyes. He wasn’t reaching for a physical handle. He was reaching for the *idea* of a handle. For the narrative function of “entrance.”

I saw him concentrate, his face a mask of intense focus. He was not a man trying to open a door. He was a reader trying to accept a premise. He was aligning his internal story with the ghost story in the air.

His fingers curled, as if around a non-existent knob.

And they *found* resistance.

A gasp escaped him. His eyes flew open. In his hand, visible only as a faint shimmer, was the shape of a doorknob. The pearlescent door was no longer just an image. It had gained a dimension of texture, of potential.

He turned his wrist.

The door swung inward, on silent, conceptual hinges.

Beyond was not the dark interior of the train station. Beyond was a corridor of pure, blinding white. Not a light, but an absence of definition. A place before the first word.

A draft.

The hook for the next chapter was not a question. It was an open parenthesis.

We stood on the threshold of the unwritten. The resonator in Alistair’s other hand was screaming, its needle shattered. The narrative tension of our quest had found its object, and the battery was spent.

We had found a seam. And we had peeled it back.

I looked at Alistair. He looked at me. There was no triumph in his face, only a terrible, solemn understanding. We were not crossing a physical boundary. We were transitioning from text to meta-text. From character to critic.

From the edited world to the place where the edits were made.

He stepped through the doorway of light.

I followed.

The world behind us—the world of rain and rubble and revised tragedies—did not disappear. It simply became one of many pages stacked on a very, very large desk.



---

# Chapter 17

## CHAPTER 17

The first thing I did after stepping into the unwritten was to check my pockets.

It was an instinct, a grounding tic from the old world. A way to prove you were still a contiguous entity, that your personal inventory hadn’t been voided by the transition. My fingers found the familiar: the worn edges of the useless receipt, the cold disc of the battery, a single, petrified peppermint from a tin I’d looted three chapters ago. The resonator was gone. Alistair’s hand, which had held it, was empty. We had left the screaming instrument behind, its purpose fulfilled. It had broken the fourth wall, and in doing so, had rendered itself obsolete. A key that melts in the lock.

The white wasn’t a void. That was my second realization. A void implies potential, an emptiness waiting to be filled. This was different. This was a *negative space*, the kind left behind when something very dense has been removed. The air wasn’t bright; it was *bleached*. It carried no scent, no temperature, only a faint, papery dryness that caught in the throat. The silence was absolute, but not peaceful. It was the silence of a held breath, of a sentence paused mid-

We stood on a surface that had no texture. It wasn’t floor. It was simply the plane on which we happened to be standing. Looking down, I saw my boots, but no shadow fell from them. The light came from everywhere and nowhere, a diffuse, source-less illumination that flattened everything.

Alistair was a statue beside me. He wasn’t looking at the expanse. He was looking at his own hands, turning them over as if seeing them for the first time. The lines on his palms seemed deeper, more like deliberate glyphs than random creases.

“The substrate,” he whispered. His voice didn’t echo. It was absorbed, a drop of ink on a blotter. “We’re in the substrate.”

I turned. The doorway we’d come through was still there, but it was no longer a pearlescent ghost in a train station wall. It was a rectangle of muted, moving colour, like a television screen playing a silent film of a rainy day. Through it, I could see the dim outline of the station platform, the fallen resonator. It looked small. Distant. Unreal. A diorama under dusty glass.

“Is this… the author’s mind?” I asked. The concept felt absurd as I said it, a child’s understanding of metaphysics.

Alistair gave a short, sharp laugh that held no humour. “No. That would be arrogant. And far too messy. This is the workshop. The editing suite. The place where the raw material of a story is sanded and fitted before it’s pushed out into the world.” He began to walk, his steps making no sound. “Follow the draft.”

I saw it then. A current. Not of air, but of *intention*. It was visible only as a slight warping of the bleached field, a river of faint turbulence moving away from the doorway. It was the narrative momentum that had carried us here, not yet dissipated. We were caught in its backwash.

We walked. Time, in that place, was not a line but a thickness. It had the quality of waiting, of something *about* to happen. The silence began to resolve into layers. Beneath the absolute quiet, I could hear—or perhaps feel—a low hum. It was the hum of potential, the white noise of every unwritten word, every unchosen path, every deleted scene. It was the sound of the story thinking about itself.

Shapes began to coalesce out of the uniformity. Not objects, but suggestions. A hazy outline of a lamppost, rendered in strokes of slightly greyer white, flickered and vanished. A flight of stairs appeared for three heartbeats, leading up into nothing, then dissolved. We were walking through the story’s memory of itself, its discarded drafts and false starts. Here, a faint, shimmering impression of a character who never was—a man with a parrot on his shoulder, sketched in two hesitant lines. There, the ghost of a subplot about a buried water main, wavering like a heat haze before being erased.

“The cutting room floor,” I said.

“The *pre*-cutting room,” Alistair corrected. “These are the thoughts that never even made it to the page. The ‘what-ifs.’ The narrative chaff.”

The current of intention was strengthening, pulling us toward a point ahead. The featureless white was beginning to show a texture, like the weave of very fine linen. And then, we saw the first solid thing.

It was a desk.

It was not a phantom or an impression. It was substantial, carved from a dark, heavy wood that drank the light. Its surface was a chaotic landscape. Stacks of paper, teetering like glacial cliffs. Coffee mugs, some holding liquid, some holding petrified sludge, one holding a collection of nib-less pens. A green-shaded banker’s lamp, unplugged, its cord snaking off the edge into the white. And books. Not the intact, bound books of our world, but carcasses of books—spines cracked open, pages splayed, margins dense with a furious, spidery script.

Behind the desk was a chair. It was empty, but it was not unoccupied. The impression of a presence lingered in its worn leather, in the particular way the left armrest was darker, smoothed by the pressure of a forearm. This was a place of work.

We had reached the source of the draft.

Alistair approached the desk with the reverence of a pilgrim at a shrine. He did not touch anything. He leaned over, peering at the topmost page on the closest stack.

His breath hitched.

I moved to his side. The page was not blank. It was filled with text, but it was a palimpsest, a chaotic overlay. The base layer was clean, typed narrative—a description of a city street after a rainstorm. Over this, in the same frantic handwriting from the margins, were revisions. Words were crossed out with violent slashes. New sentences were crammed in between lines, arrows looping them into place. Whole paragraphs were bracketed with question marks. In the top margin, written in all caps and circled three times, was the phrase: **TOO ON THE NOSE? CHECK RHYTHM.**

“Edits,” I breathed.

“Not just edits,” Alistair said, his finger hovering over a stricken passage. “Look.”

The original text read: *The city wept, its tears washing the grime from its stone face.*
The handwritten correction read: *Cliché. The city didn’t weep. It *steamed*. Its wounds hissed in the cool air.*

I knew that line. It was from Chapter 4. The description of the morning after the first major revision-storm. I had walked through that steaming city.

“We’re not in the mind of the author,” Alistair said, wonder and horror dawning in equal measure. “We’re in the manuscript. The *working* manuscript. This…” He gestured at the desk, the pages, the silent, presiding chair. “This is the interface.”

A cold understanding settled in my gut. We weren’t behind the curtain. We were in the marginalia. We were the words being scrutinized, circled, and potentially deleted.

The draft—the current that had led us here—was flowing from the chair, past the desk, and into the white. It was the outflow of creative decision. I followed its path with my eyes. It streamed toward a distant, vertical line of deeper white. A seam.

We moved past the desk, drawn toward it. As we neared, the seam resolved into a tall, narrow portal, identical to the one we’d entered. Through it, I could see another bleached space, another desk, another chaotic landscape of paper. But the details were different. The mug held pencils. The handwriting on a visible page was neater, more measured.

“Another chapter,” Alistair said. “Another node in the edit.”

We passed through the portal. The transition was seamless. One editing suite bled into another. Here, the marginalia was less frantic. The comments were things like **“Pacing?”** and **“Foreshadowing for Ch. 19?”**. We saw a page where a description of Marnie’s cardigan had been trimmed from five lines to two. A note in the margin read: **She is defined by action, not wardrobe. Tighten.**

We moved on. Portal to portal, suite to suite. It was a labyrinth of the story’s own becoming. We saw the brutal, early revisions—whole pages blacked out, characters introduced and then killed in the margin with a succinct **“No.”** We saw the middle chapters, where the prose was polished, the metaphors sharpened. **“Better,”** a note would say. Or, **“Echo ‘pigeon’s underbelly’ here. Thread it.”**

We were walking our own timeline backwards in its raw, unformed state. It was a profoundly vulnerable feeling. To see the hesitation, the second-guessing, the outright rejection that had shaped the ground we walked on. Our tragedies, our narrow escapes, our moments of bleak humour—they were all here, not as lived experience, but as *choices*. Options weighed and measured.

In the suite for what I guessed was Chapter 11, we found a different kind of artifact. Stuck to a page with a gummed label was a small, rectangular slip—a real-world bookmark for a library book. On it, in faded ink, was a handwritten list:
* milk
* stamps
* call mum
* *the melancholy of abandoned places*

The mundane shopping list, juxtaposed with the thematic note. The author’s life, seeping into the text. It was the most human thing I’d seen since we arrived, and it made the place feel infinitely more lonely.

“They’re not a god,” I said, staring at the bookmark. “They’re just a person. At a desk.”

“A person with a red pen,” Alistair replied grimly. He was looking at a stack of pages for a chapter ahead of us. I tried to glimpse the page numbers, but they were obscured by a large, hand-drawn diagram—a timeline with intersecting arrows and question marks. A plot map. One arrow, thick and dark, pointed to a chapter number circled in red: 24.

The draft was pulling us forward again, toward the present, toward the chapters still wet with ink. We passed through a portal and the atmosphere changed. The hum of potential was louder here, edged with a static of imminent activity. The pages on the desk were crisp, the ink darker. The notes were urgent. **“PAY THIS OFF”** was scrawled across two pages, with a line connecting a line of dialogue from Chapter 9 to a blank space in Chapter 22.

And then, in the suite for Chapter 16—*our* chapter, the one we had just left—we saw it.

The page was open to the final scene. Our dialogue was there. My observations. Alistair’s theory about the narrative substrate. The description of the pearlescent door. The text ended with my line: *I followed.*

But below that, the page was blank. The story stopped.

Except it didn’t.

In the margin, the handwriting was fresh. The ink seemed to gleam. It was a note, not a revision. A directive.

**They’re in the manuscript now. Good. The battery is spent. The resonator’s purpose is served. But the seam they found… it’s unstable. A side-effect of the edit. It will try to close.**
**The tension cannot collapse here. They need a new catalyst.**
**Introduce the Correction.**

Alistair and I read the words at the same moment. A new kind of cold, deeper than the bleach-white silence, locked my joints.

“The Correction?” I said.

Before he could answer, the hum in the room shifted. The static crackle intensified. From the direction of the portal we’d entered—the one leading back to the train station diorama—a sound began to intrude. Not the white noise of potential, but a specific, grinding, *unmaking* sound. The sound of a large eraser on heavy paper.

We turned.

The portal was changing. The rectangle of muted, rainy colour was shrinking. Not like a door closing, but like a hole being patched. The edges were curling inward, dissolving into the white, sealing themselves shut. The view of our world was being edited out.

The seam was closing.

“The unstable side-effect,” Alistair said, his voice tight. “It’s resolving. The narrative is smoothing itself over. Erasing the anomaly.”

Panic, pure and electric, shot through me. That portal was our only known way back. It was our tether to the world of rain and rubble, however revised. To Marnie and her rules, to the broken city, to the tangible weight of a useless receipt in my pocket.

“Run,” I said.

We ran. Not back toward the sealing portal—that was futile—but forward, following the intensifying draft, deeper into the manuscript’s present tense. We burst through the next portal just as the grinding sound behind us reached a crescendo and then stopped, replaced by a perfect, seamless silence. I glanced back. The portal was gone. Only the uniform, textured white remained. Our exit had been deleted.

We were trapped in the edit.

We stumbled into the next suite. This one was for a chapter not yet written. The desk was mostly clear, save for a single, blank sheet of paper centered under the green lamp. The draft here was strong, a river of intent pulling toward the next blank portal. But the room was not empty.

Standing by the desk, her back to us, was a woman.

She was tall, dressed in a severe grey suit that seemed woven from the same substance as the walls. Her hair was pulled into a tight, flawless bun. She held a clipboard. She was not a phantom or a memory. She was as solid as the desk. She was studying the blank page.

At the sound of our entrance, she turned.

Her face was pleasant, but in a way that was utterly empty. It was a default expression, like that of a mannequin or a newscaster reading a tragedy. Her eyes were the colour of the bleached white around us.

“Ah,” she said. Her voice was calm, clear, and carried a faint, digital flatness. “The anomalies.”

She consulted her clipboard. “Chapter 16 bleed-through. Designated: ‘The Protagonist’ and ‘The Theorist.’” She looked up. “You are out of place.”

“Who are you?” Alistair demanded, stepping slightly in front of me.

“I am the Correction,” she said, as if stating the time. “A narrative immune response. You have entered a sterile field. You are contaminants. You must be removed.”

“We didn’t mean to,” I said. “We were following a thread. A seam.”

“The seam was an error,” the Correction replied. “A loose thread in the narrative weave. It has been clipped. Your presence here is a compounding error. You are disrupting the workflow.” She took a step toward us. Her movements were efficient, economical. “You will be deleted.”

She didn’t raise a weapon. She simply lifted a hand, index finger extended. She pointed at Alistair.

Nothing visible shot from her finger. But Alistair gasped, clutching his chest. He staggered back. The colour drained from his face, not into pallor, but into something else—into a faint transparency. I could see, just for a second, the bleached white of the wall behind him, shimmering through his body.

“Stop!” I shouted, throwing myself between them.

The Correction’s blank eyes shifted to me. “You are a point-of-view violation. A first-person incursion into a third-person administrative space. You are non-viable.”

Her finger began to turn toward me.

Alistair, still shimmering, grabbed my arm. “The draft!” he choked out. “Follow the current! It’s the only thing she doesn’t control!”

He was right. The Correction stood apart from the river of intention flowing from the desk to the next portal. She was static, a function, not a part of the story’s flow.

We ran. Not with the grace of characters in an action scene, but with the desperate, scrambling panic of real creatures trying to avoid erasure. We plunged toward the next portal.

The Correction did not run. She simply turned and began to walk after us, her heels making no sound on the non-floor. Her pace was steady, unhurried. She was a process. She would complete her task.

We burst through into the next suite. Chapter 18, perhaps. Here, there were a few lines of text on the page, a opening description of the white space itself. It was being written in real time. As we watched, words formed on the page, typing themselves out from no visible source:

*They ran, the bleach-white silence swallowing the sound of their panic—*

The sentence stopped. A cursor blinked.

Then, the handwriting—the author’s hand—scrawled in the margin: **No. Too direct. Show, don’t tell their panic. Maybe through the metaphor of the closing seam?**

The typed words vanished, deleted.

The Correction stepped through the portal behind us. She was closer now.

“You cannot outrun a function,” she stated. “You exist only where you are permitted. This space does not permit you.”

We ran again. Portal to portal. We were rabbits fleeing through the warren of the story’s own construction. In each suite, we saw fragments of what was to come—a line of dialogue, a setting, a symbolic object. A knife on a velvet cloth. A room full of stopped clocks. A garden of glass flowers. They were glimpses of our future, laid out in disassembled parts, waiting to be assembled into experience.

And always, the Correction followed. Walking. Pointing. With each pointing, a piece of us grew fainter. A memory of the taste of real coffee dissolved from my mind. The specific feeling of the rain on my face in Chapter 3 grew vague. Alistair lost the memory of his mentor’s name. We were being unmade not from the outside in, but from the inside out. Our backstory was being deleted.

We stumbled into a larger suite. The draft here was a torrent, a roaring river of intent. The desk was massive, piled high with maps and timelines. This was a pivotal node. The page on the desk was dense with text. I saw the number at the top: 23.

We were nearing the end.

And the Correction was at the portal behind us, her finger rising.

“There!” Alistair yelled, his voice thin, like a radio signal fading.

He wasn’t pointing at the next portal. He was pointing at the desk. At the manuscript page for Chapter 23. In the middle of a paragraph, there was a gap. A place where the typed text stopped, and the handwritten note said: **[[BRIDGE NEEDED HERE. CONNECTION TO FINAL THEME. SOMETHING ABOUT THE RECEIPT?]]**

A blank space. A narrative lacuna.

An idea, desperate and insane, struck me. The Correction was a function of the edited manuscript. She operated on the text as it was *meant* to be. She was deleting anomalies.

What if we stopped being anomalies?

“The blank!” I shouted at Alistair. “We have to get into the blank!”

He understood instantly. We were out of place in the edited text. But a blank space was a place for new text. For possibilities. It was the one place in this sterile field where something unexpected might be allowed to exist, if only for a moment.

The Correction’s finger pointed. A wave of nullity washed toward us.

We dove. Not for a portal, but for the surface of the desk, for the open page of Chapter 23. We reached for the blank space in the paragraph, the gap marked by the handwritten brackets.

It was like diving into a pool of static. The world of the editing suite—the desk, the lamp, the walking Correction—rippled, distorted, and then…

…re-formed.

The bleached white was gone. The papery dryness was gone.

We were kneeling on cold, wet cobblestones. The air was thick with the smell of ozone and rotting flowers. A familiar, fungal light seeped from a bruised sky.

We were back in the world. But not our world.

Or rather, not a *finished* version of it.

We were in the blank space. In the bridge that needed writing.

Everything around us was… provisional. The buildings had a sketched quality, their edges slightly fuzzy, as if the ink hadn’t fully dried. Colours were muted, unsure of themselves. A lamppost flickered between being wrought iron and simple wood. The sound of distant water was either a river or a broken main, the audio track undecided.

I looked at my hands. They were solid. The transparency was gone. Alistair, beside me, was panting, whole again. Our deleted memories did not return, but the erosion had stopped.

We had escaped the Correction by throwing ourselves into a hole in the narrative. We had become a potential bridge.

I stood up on the shaky cobblestones. In the distance, at the end of the unreal street, I could see the solid, dark outline of a familiar structure—the train station. The real one. The one made of stone and consequence. We were close to where we started, but in a layer of the story that was still under construction.

The hook for the next chapter was no longer an open parenthesis.

It was a blank line, waiting to be filled.

And we were the words that had to fill it.

Alistair got to his feet, wincing as if his bones were remembering their proper density. He looked at the flickering, half-formed world around us, then at the solid station in the distance.

“The bridge,” he said, his voice raw. “We’re the bridge. She’ll still come. She’s a function. She’ll follow the continuity error into the blank.”

I nodded, the weight of it settling. We had bought time, not safety. The Correction would come. She would step out of the manuscript and into this unmade alley, her grey suit a stark slash in the fuzzy air.

We had to make this blank space into something before she arrived. We had to write our own escape into the solid text ahead.

I reached into my pocket. My fingers closed around the receipt. The last artifact. The original, mundane motivation.

It was crisp. It was real.

And in the margin of Chapter 23, the author had wondered about it.

I held it up. The pale paper, the faded ink of the register, the date from a dead world.

“The connection to the final theme,” I said.

The world around us seemed to lean in, the unfixed details trembling, waiting for the next word.

We had to build a bridge from a blank space, using a receipt, before the eraser caught up to us.

The unwritten was now our only ground. And we had to write it ourselves.



---

# Chapter 18

## CHAPTER 18

The blank space was not quiet. It hummed. A low, sub-auditory thrumming, like the memory of a refrigerator in a vacant apartment. The air tasted of ozone and unprinted paper. The cobblestones under our feet were a suggestion, their edges bleeding into a fuzzy, grey non-colour that wasn’t quite mist. Ahead, the train station was a lithograph, stark and immutable. Behind us, the hole we’d torn—a ragged parenthesis of nothing—gaped in the air, a wound in the story’s skin.

We were the infection in that wound. A continuity error. And she was the story’s white blood cell.

“We don’t build a bridge,” I said, my voice swallowed by the humming void. “We *are* the bridge. The narrative tension. The thing that connects the blank to the solid.” I looked at the receipt in my hand. It was the only object here that felt entirely real, its corners sharp enough to cut the fuzziness around it. “This is the anchor. The first sentence. ‘The first thing I did after the world ended was to try and return a sweater.’ This receipt is the proof of purchase. It’s the original motivation.”

Alistair stared at the distant station. His face, in this half-light, was a study in remembered pain. “A motivation for what? To get a store credit in a currency that doesn’t exist? To argue with a ghost in a moth-eaten cardigan?” He kicked a loose cobble. It didn’t clatter; it made a sound like a dropped book, then dissolved into a scatter of pencil-sketched fragments before reforming. “It’s a receipt, Leo. It’s a scrap. It’s the past.”

“That’s the point,” I said, a desperate idea cohering. “The lens. *Past future*. We’ve been reading it wrong. It’s not about the future of the past. It’s about the *past of the future*. What comes before the thing that hasn’t happened yet. The cause that precedes the effect still in draft. This receipt…” I held it up, and the unfixed world seemed to tilt on its axis, the grey bleeding toward a specific, pigeon-underbelly shade. “This is the past of my future. The reason I’m here, in this blank, instead of safely deleted. She can’t correct a motivation. She can only correct events. We have to make the motivation the plot.”

The air behind us rippled. A familiar, dreadful solidity began to press against the soft edges of our blank. A grey hemline, precise as a scalpel, appeared in the tear.

“She’s found the thread,” Alistair whispered.

The Correction was stepping through.

We didn’t run toward the station. Running implied solid ground. We *leaned* forward, a deliberate act of will against the resisting mush of the unwritten. With each step, the world defined itself a little more. A lamppost flickered into being, then a second, casting a light that had no source. The cobblestones gained weight, their grout filling with the ghost of mortar. We were writing our path by walking it, but it was slow, so slow.

She was faster.

She emerged from the tear not with a dramatic rupture, but with the quiet inevitability of a corrected typo. One moment the blank was blank; the next, she was in it, her grey suit a brutal declaration of fact against the maybe-stuff of this place. She didn’t look at the flickering lampposts or the unstable stones. She looked at us. At the discontinuity we represented.

“Subject Leo,” she said, her voice the dry rustle of pages being turned to find an error. “Subject Alistair. Narrative anomaly. You have exited the authorised text.”

“We’ve entered the margin,” I said, not stopping. The station was closer now. I could see the soot stains on the limestone, the missing ‘R’ in ‘ARRIVALS’. Real details. Consequence.

“The margin is not for characters,” she stated, beginning to walk. Her steps didn’t coax the world into being; they *imposed* it. Where her heels met the fuzzy ground, it became solid, grey linoleum, spreading like a stain. She was writing over our fledgling reality with her own. A correction in real-time. “The margin is for notes. For questions. You are an answer that does not fit the question.”

Alistair was breathing in ragged gasps. “She’s turning the path behind her into her world. She’s erasing our trail as she follows it.”

He was right. The lampposts we’d sparked were going out, one by one, snuffed as her domain of sterile certainty touched them. The humming was being replaced by a dead, library silence. She was a rolling tide of final draft, and we were the first draft scrawled on a napkin, about to be balled up.

The receipt. It was burning a hole in my palm.

I stopped, turning to face her. Alistair grabbed my arm. “Leo, no, we’re almost—”

“We won’t make it,” I said. “She’s a function. She’s faster in a straight line. We have to change the genre.”

I held the receipt up, not to her, but to the blank space around us. To the potential. I didn’t shout. I recited.

“Item: one cashmere V-neck. Colour: dove grey. Price: eighty-nine fifty. Payment method: card ending in four-five-one-two. Date: October seventeenth. Reason for return: colour not as expected.”

The Correction paused. Her head tilted a precise three degrees. “Irrelevant data. Consumer transaction protocols are non-operational. The referenced vendor is null.”

But the blank space reacted. The fuzzy grey coalesced, not into linoleum, but into a floor—veined marble, cracked and dusty. Walls flickered: plaster and lathe, then chrome and glass, then rough brick. It was trying to be the department store. It was trying to build the past of my future from the receipt’s metadata.

“It’s not irrelevant,” I said, walking backward, pulling Alistair with me, my eyes locked on hers. “It’s the premise. The first cause. You can delete me, but you can’t delete why I started. The author wondered about this. In the margin of Chapter Twenty-Three. They asked, ‘Why the receipt? What’s the connection?’ You don’t know the answer. You can’t correct a question.”

For the first time, I saw a micro-expression on her face. Not confusion. Processing delay. A split-second buffer as her function parsed an illogical input. A character citing marginal authorial intent was a paradox. She was a tool for enforcing internal consistency; she had no protocol for meta-textual critique.

The moment lasted less than a second. But in that second, the world around us *crystallised*.

Not as the department store. Not as the alley.

As the bridge.

It erupted from the marble-fuzzy floor, a span of iron and memory, arcing over a chasm that hadn’t been there a breath before. The chasm wasn’t empty. It swirled with the ghosts of deleted text, with half-formed images and abandoned plotlines—a screaming face here, a burning tree there, all melting into a soup of almost-was. The far end of the bridge was anchored in the solid, sooty stone of the train station’s platform. The near end was here, under our feet, wrought from the receipt’s data-stream: invoice numbers became rivets, the barcode became a railing, the clerk’s scrawled initials became a rust-streaked plaque.

We stood on a bridge made of a mundane financial transaction. It was absurd. It was perfect.

The Correction stood at the edge of the chasm, her grey linoleum reality ending in a sharp line. She looked at the bridge. She looked at the chasm of deleted potential. Her function was to delete errors and connect narrative gaps with sanctioned prose. This was a gap. We had built a connection. But the building material was unauthorised.

Her hesitation was our victory. False, fragile, and fleeting—but a victory.

“Go!” I shoved Alistair onto the bridge.

The iron groaned underfoot, but it held. It felt real—cold, gritty, shuddering with a vibration that seemed to come from the chasm below. We ran. Not the desperate, floundering run through the blank, but a pounding, echoing sprint on a defined surface. The station platform grew closer, its details snapping into horrifying focus: the peeling green paint of a bench, a discarded newspaper headline bleached by sun and time, the smell of damp coal and old urine.

I dared a glance back.

The Correction was stepping onto the bridge.

She didn’t run. She walked. And with each step, the bridge changed. The iron under her feet smoothed, turned a uniform, institutional grey. The rust vanished. The quirky, receipt-born imperfections were sanded away, replaced by a featureless, efficient walkway. She was correcting the bridge itself, transforming our absurd, personal span into a bland, functional overpass. She was making it hers.

And she was gaining. Her progress was a wave of erasure, swallowing the bridge’s character foot by foot.

Alistair reached the platform and hauled himself over the edge. I was three steps behind. The Correction was twenty steps back, but her wave of grey was only ten steps behind me, moving faster than she was. The bridge was becoming a sterile tube, the chasm below fading into a neutral white void.

I threw myself onto the platform just as the grey reached the end of the bridge. The final foot of iron—the part anchored in the station’s reality—resisted. It was real stone, real mortar. The correction stuttered, like a wave hitting a seawall.

For a moment, we were safe on the solid ground of the established story. The station was exactly as we’d left it a lifetime ago, yet utterly different. It was emptier. The silence was total, a held breath. The posters on the walls were blank. The destination board showed no times, no cities. It was a stage set after the play has ended.

We lay on the cold platform, gasping. The bridge, now a smooth grey ramp leading from the platform’s edge into a white nothing, began to dissolve. The Correction stood at its terminus, on the very last inch of corrected material. She could not step off. The rules were clear: she operated on errors, on inconsistencies. The station, for all its eerie emptiness, was canon. It was in Chapter One. It was real.

She watched us, her hands at her sides. “The anomaly is contained,” she said, her voice carrying perfectly across the short, impossible distance. “You have returned to the authorised narrative. Your deletion is now a matter of textual housekeeping.”

She didn’t move. She didn’t need to. She was a function. And the story, this original, solid story, had its own functions.

A train whistle sounded, a long, mournful note that seemed to come from the stones themselves.

Down the track, a single headlight appeared, cutting through the stagnant air.

The 11:15 to Oblivion. Or whatever its destination was now.

It was coming. And it would stop at this platform. And we would have to get on. Because that was what happened in this station. That was the plot.

The Correction’s lips—a pale, bloodless line—twitched. Not a smile. A semantic satisfaction. “The narrative imperative will resolve the anomaly. You will board. You will be transported to a narrative terminus. Efficient. Clean.”

Alistair pushed himself to his knees. “We beat her here. We made it back.”

“No,” I said, the cold of the platform seeping into my bones. “We didn’t beat her. We led her to the one place where she doesn’t have to do the work herself. We’re back in the manuscript. And the manuscript has a plot. A plot that kills us.” I looked at the oncoming light, growing larger, brighter. “The false victory. Escalation. We thought the bridge was the win. It was just the delivery mechanism.”

The train was a dark slug against the lighter dark of the tunnel, its single eye now blinding. It wasn’t slowing down. It was barreling into the station with a terrifying, mechanical certainty.

We scrambled back from the edge. The train shrieked past, a hurricane of noise and forced air, and squealed to a stop with a finality that shook the platform. The doors, tall and black and without windows, sighed open.

Empty. A cavernous, lit emptiness.

The invitation was absolute.

The Correction watched, a curator at the edge of a diorama.

This was the collapse. The bridge we built, the blank we wrote, it had all just been a convoluted way to walk ourselves back onto the page that contained our death sentence. The plot demanded it. The past future demanded it. The cause (our escape) had led to the effect (our return to the lethal narrative) that was always waiting.

I stood up. My legs were water. I looked at the open train doors, then back at the Correction, a statue of bureaucratic finality. I looked at Alistair, his face hollow with the same understanding.

Then I looked down at my hand.

The receipt was gone.

I had it on the bridge. I felt its crispness. Now, my pocket was empty.

“The anchor,” I breathed.

“What?” Alistair said, his eyes fixed on the waiting train.
